  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3a4b9b4b-f025-4b62-9f5b-da07fa9528ef'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer1235514483951019287">The discovery of a mysterious notebook turns a man's life upside-down in this compulsively readable novel by 'one of the great writers of our time' ( San Francisco Chronicle ).<br/><br/>Several months into his recovery from a near-fatal illness, novelist Sidney Orr enters a stationery shop in Brooklyn and buys a blue notebook. It is September 18, 1982, and for the next nine days Orr</span>
  <span id="freeText1235514483951019287" style="display:none">The discovery of a mysterious notebook turns a man's life upside-down in this compulsively readable novel by 'one of the great writers of our time' ( San Francisco Chronicle ).<br /><br />Several months into his recovery from a near-fatal illness, novelist Sidney Orr enters a stationery shop in Brooklyn and buys a blue notebook. It is September 18, 1982, and for the next nine days Orr will live under the spell of this blank book, trapped inside a world of eerie premonitions and bewildering events that threaten to destroy his marriage and undermine his faith in reality.<br /><br />Paul Auster's mesmerizing eleventh novel reads like an old-fashioned ghost story. But there are no ghosts in this book - only flesh-and-blood human beings, wandering through the haunted realms of everyday life. Oracle Night is a narrative tour de force that confirms Auster's reputation as one of the boldest, most original writers at work in America today.</span>

<div id="review_34290750" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34290750" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34290750?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789643691639" href="/book/show/4962842">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34290750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7959740804973197962">Oracle Night, Paul Auster<br/>The novel is about a writer named Sidney Orr (Orlovsky), who, after making a miraculous recovery from near fatal illness, buys a new notebook and starts writing a story about a man who completely changed his life when he realized how much his existence was ruled by randomness.<br/><br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه نوامبر سال 2003 میلادی<br/>عنوان: شب پیشگویی؛ نویسنده: پل آستر (اوستر)؛ مترجم: خجسته کیهان؛ تهران، افق، 1384؛ در 271 ص؛ فروست: ادبیات امروز، رمان 23؛ شابک: 9643691632؛ چاپ دوم 138</span>
  <span id="freeText7959740804973197962" style="display:none">Oracle Night, Paul Auster<br />The novel is about a writer named Sidney Orr (Orlovsky), who, after making a miraculous recovery from near fatal illness, buys a new notebook and starts writing a story about a man who completely changed his life when he realized how much his existence was ruled by randomness.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه نوامبر سال 2003 میلادی<br />عنوان: شب پیشگویی؛ نویسنده: پل آستر (اوستر)؛ مترجم: خجسته کیهان؛ تهران، افق، 1384؛ در 271 ص؛ فروست: ادبیات امروز، رمان 23؛ شابک: 9643691632؛ چاپ دوم 1386؛ چاپ پنجم 1392؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 21 م<br /><br />داستان «شب پیشگویی» روایت زندگی جوان نویسنده ای ست که پس از مدتی دوری از نوشتن؛ با خریدن یک دفترچه ی آبی رنگ بار دیگر اشتیاق نوشتن را در خود احساس میکند، و با طرح داستانی در ذهنش آغازبه نگارش داستان میکند؛ داستان مردی که یک زندگی را ترک میکند و ناپدید میشود، تا در جایی دیگر، زندگی دیگری برای خود بسازد...؛ جوان نویسنده که به تازگی پس از گذراندن یک بیماری دشوار و کشنده، بهبود یافته، در کنار داستانی که آغاز کرده، در کنار داستان زندگی خویش، به روایت داستان همسرش نیز میپردازد؛ و این سبب ایجاد چالشهایی در زندگی مشترک نویسنده با همسرش میشود. نویسنده ی جوان برای پرداختن به بخشهای پنهان زندگی مشترکشان، خیالپردازی میکند، و داستان را پیش میبرد...؛ نقل از کتاب: «ما عاشق بدنها نمیشویم، بلکه گرفتار عشق یکدیگر میشویم، و اگرچه موجودیت ما در پوست و استخوان محدود است، ولی همه چیز در آن خلاصه نمیشود. همه این را میدانیم، با اینحال به محض اینکه از فهرست خصوصیات ظاهری فراتر میرویم، درمیمانیم و واژه ها در سردرگمی و استعاره های مه آلود و بی اساس مچاله میشوند. بعضیها آنرا آتش وجود مینامند. برخی شعله ی درونی یا پرتو درونی وجود، و بعضی دیگر آن را گوهر فروزان درون مینامند. این اصطلاحات، همیشه تصاویر نور، گرما و نیرو را تداعی میکنند. آن جوهر زندگی که گاه روح مینامیم، همواره از طریق چشم با دیگران ارتباط مییابد. پافشاری شاعران بر این نکته حتما درست است. راز اشتیاق با نگریستن به چشمان محبوب آغاز میشود، چون تنها در آنجاست که میتوان لحظه ای به آنچه که او واقعا هست، پی برد...»؛ پایان نقل. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="7959740804973197962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10634238616817069132"><i>I had been sick for a long time. When the day came for me to leave the hospital, I barely knew how to walk anymore, could barely remember who I was supposed to be....</i><br/><br/>One of the best Austers I've read, seriously. <br/>Auster, you love him or you hate him. I've struggled with his books, but always ended up fascinated. <br/>Auster writes brooding stories, there's always something below the surface and to find out what's going on is a challenge....This is a brooding story too, it was a smooth read for me, enj</span>
  <span id="freeText10634238616817069132" style="display:none"><i>I had been sick for a long time. When the day came for me to leave the hospital, I barely knew how to walk anymore, could barely remember who I was supposed to be....</i><br /><br />One of the best Austers I've read, seriously. <br />Auster, you love him or you hate him. I've struggled with his books, but always ended up fascinated. <br />Auster writes brooding stories, there's always something below the surface and to find out what's going on is a challenge....This is a brooding story too, it was a smooth read for me, enjoyed every moment of it. Brooding, fascinating, intriguing, loving &amp; sad. Fantastic story, really. Auster uses storylines within the main story, by way of footer notes in small print. Yes, it worked for me. And again, I love Auster's usual setting, New York, Brooklyn. The main character in the book Sidney Orr, walks the streets of New York, recovering from a serious illness. I can see, smell and feel New York.... one of my favorite cities in the world.... Auster always knows how to describe it just in the right way.... Loved it. 4.5 stars, maybe five. It's a story that lingers in the back of your head....<br />Recommended. <br /><br />The story in short: <i>"Several months into his recovery from a near-fatal illness 34-old novelist Sidney Orr enters a stationery shop in Brooklyn and buys a blue notebook. For the next 9 days Orr will live under the spell of this blue book, trapped inside a world of eerie premonitions and bewildering events that threaten to destroy his marriage and undermine his faith in reality..."</i></span>

<div id="review_128434946" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128434946" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128434946?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128434946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5323605776201039132">The story-within-the-story device gives Paul Auster plenty of freedom to orchestrate this postmodern novel. These are the (fake) jottings of an active writer and they include daily occurrences (diarylike), ideas for plots, a coherent free-flow of thoughts and musings, a scrapbook of so much mixed media which describes a vivid world dabbling in the surreal. His avoiding the usage of quotation marks in the story-within-the-story’s dialogue conveys the continuity of the writer’s imagination. He use</span>
  <span id="freeText5323605776201039132" style="display:none">The story-within-the-story device gives Paul Auster plenty of freedom to orchestrate this postmodern novel. These are the (fake) jottings of an active writer and they include daily occurrences (diarylike), ideas for plots, a coherent free-flow of thoughts and musings, a scrapbook of so much mixed media which describes a vivid world dabbling in the surreal. His avoiding the usage of quotation marks in the story-within-the-story’s dialogue conveys the continuity of the writer’s imagination. He uses literary irony, sometimes his character’s descriptions are so remarkably like those he makes of his acquaintances. Plot-wise there is jumping around, making whole days lapse only one paragraph, creating different story lines and times, using repetition to give the reader a vague sense of déjà vu. <br /><br />Chapterless, it continues in the major tradition of the thriller (continued &amp; realized to its most masterful level in 2006 with Scott Smith’s novel of visceral horror The Ruins) to be easy-to-follow, despite having no rests between climaxes &amp; plot revelations. The “narcissistic novel” brand is befitting of 'Oracle Night.'<br />	<br />Let’s not forget Auster's trademark use of footnotes. More than creating an “academic” (i.e. entitled) voice, this tool can pump in more information to an otherwise “short” novel. Footnotes are small pockets of compressed data; additionally they allow the reader to interact with the text itself. He flips the pages prematurely to get the "hidden" back story, breaking the rules of literature by traversing through the main plot itself...<br /><br />This is a book about books through &amp; through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5323605776201039132" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1742733574" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1742733574" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1742733574?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1742733574" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3588981653257232447">Audiobook... read by Paul Auster<br/><br/>This was my 3rd time listening to Auster read one of his books. His voice is distinct, engaging, natural, terrific rhythmic flow... complementing his storytelling prose. <br/><br/>I’m late to seriously becoming a fan... but, I’m definitely getting there. <br/>Auster really holds my attention - so much so - I promised myself that the next Auster book I choose - I’ll read it rather than listen to him. <br/>I have a hard time separating the entertainment value between his voice or the s</span>
  <span id="freeText3588981653257232447" style="display:none">Audiobook... read by Paul Auster<br /><br />This was my 3rd time listening to Auster read one of his books. His voice is distinct, engaging, natural, terrific rhythmic flow... complementing his storytelling prose. <br /><br />I’m late to seriously becoming a fan... but, I’m definitely getting there. <br />Auster really holds my attention - so much so - I promised myself that the next Auster book I choose - I’ll read it rather than listen to him. <br />I have a hard time separating the entertainment value between his voice or the story itself. I like both! <br /><br />We meet Sidney Orr - a novelist... and the narrator. <br />Sidney was recovering from a serious illness. After leaving the hospital he walks into “The Paper Palace”, a stationary store and bought a blue notebook that was made in Portugal. <br />He becomes obsessed it - and he begins to write again after a period of writers block. <br />Interesting odd things start to happen as Sid begins to write.<br /><br />This is a complex story about a book of stories, ( three to be specific), within stories...connecting them to all to Sidney. ( and all the things important in his own life). <br /><br />The mystery, intrigue, and descriptions kept me glued.<br /><br />This is a novel but it times it felt like it was non-fiction. <br />Set in 1982, we follow Sydney‘s life for nine days. His marriage is threatened... coincidences happened... characters are compelling...( especially Grace, his wife)...<br />and we take an introspective look into the future. <br /><br />For me - the greatness was the intimacy of Sidney ‘telling’ the stories. ( leaving hints of the concerns of his own life). <br /><br />That old saying, “I could listen to this guy read the phone book”, applies accurately for me with Paul Auster. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3588981653257232447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6324137107597010930">Every time I start a Paul Auster book I get twenty pages into it and think,<br/>"That's it. He's finally over stepped the mark. He's jumped from the edge of the clever cliff and into the precipice of w*nky too smart and arty for his own good literature". And then, I am always proved wrong. Sorry Paul I judge you too harshly!*<br/><br/>Another excellent book with the trademark Austerisms. Convoluted, random, perhaps even a little too common place at times, writhing like a bag of snakes the twists and turns had </span>
  <span id="freeText6324137107597010930" style="display:none">Every time I start a Paul Auster book I get twenty pages into it and think,<br />"That's it. He's finally over stepped the mark. He's jumped from the edge of the clever cliff and into the precipice of w*nky too smart and arty for his own good literature". And then, I am always proved wrong. Sorry Paul I judge you too harshly!*<br /><br />Another excellent book with the trademark Austerisms. Convoluted, random, perhaps even a little too common place at times, writhing like a bag of snakes the twists and turns had me on the ceiling and facing the wrong way before I knew it. This either means they were very good or that I am easily confused, I'll leave it to you to decide which.<br /><br />*Although as a footnote (something that you seem to be all too fond of in this book), I just thought it was worth pointing out that as a personal preference it really winds me up when the footnotes are actually larger than the main text. Anyone else feel this way? Also if the footnote stretches for more than three pages and is running as a sort of contemporaneous commentary then it's probably worth just scrapping the footnotes and putting all the information into the main text... non? See, like here... I could have just put this bit of the review up there with the main part of the review but now you're down here reading this part of the review and by the time you glance back up to the main thread you'll have forgotten what I was talking about up there and be thinking about the footnote which is really just the story continued in another way. Hmmm. So is it annoying or is it just me?</span>

<div id="review_148365651" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148365651" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605586833p2/869064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/148365651?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148365651" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1503209140936370480">Oracle Night seems like one of Auster's more perfunctory novels. There is the usual blend of a narrator getting over some big personal tragedy, reflections on the power of language (writing especially in this case), recurring coincidences, a female love interest in trouble, etc. The opening conceit of the blank notebook and Sid's need to fill it has this really interesting, ominous vibe going to it. But Auster doesn't seem all that committed to really diving into it, and by the end of the book i</span>
  <span id="freeText1503209140936370480" style="display:none">Oracle Night seems like one of Auster's more perfunctory novels. There is the usual blend of a narrator getting over some big personal tragedy, reflections on the power of language (writing especially in this case), recurring coincidences, a female love interest in trouble, etc. The opening conceit of the blank notebook and Sid's need to fill it has this really interesting, ominous vibe going to it. But Auster doesn't seem all that committed to really diving into it, and by the end of the book it felt like little more than a forgotten pretext to set the whole narrative up instead of an organic development within the story. There are some really nice passages though, and it's interesting to read one of his books that has a decidedly more domestic kind of feel to it than a lot of his other work. But the ending feels rushed, and the whole strung-out-son-of-a-family-friend who suddenly pops up to wreak havoc in everyone's lives feels kind of like a cheap deus ex machina. I'd expect that kind of weak stuff from someone's first novel, not their umpteenth.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1503209140936370480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3414521672" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3414521672" />
    <a title="Franky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2534471-franky"><img alt="Franky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312356219p2/2534471.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3414521672?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Franky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Franky" href="/user/show/2534471-franky">Franky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312428952" href="/book/show/6457372-oracle-night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=2020-reading-challenge">2020-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=story-within-a-story">story-within-a-story</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2534471-franky?shelf=blue-notebook">blue-notebook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3414521672" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11919680982456194178">I read one of Paul Auster’s works, The New York Trilogy, a few years back, enjoyed it immensely, and it was good to get back to reading him again. I think what Auster manages to do in both of the works I’ve read is create a sense of layers of meaning in both the real world and the literary world (the one being written on paper). Both in The Trilogy and Oracle Night, there’s a sense of metacognition or thinking about thinking, or thinking and reflection on the world. Events, people, conflict play</span>
  <span id="freeText11919680982456194178" style="display:none">I read one of Paul Auster’s works, The New York Trilogy, a few years back, enjoyed it immensely, and it was good to get back to reading him again. I think what Auster manages to do in both of the works I’ve read is create a sense of layers of meaning in both the real world and the literary world (the one being written on paper). Both in The Trilogy and Oracle Night, there’s a sense of metacognition or thinking about thinking, or thinking and reflection on the world. Events, people, conflict play out and have many layers to them. In short, he is an author that makes you think and that is what I’ve loved about reading his books so far. <br /><br />I will have to do the calculations, but I think Auster’s book might at some points cross over into a “story within a story within a story.” We have Paul Auster writing about his protagonist Sidney Orr, a writer who is working on a manuscript about a man named Nick Bowen who, in this book, is focused on reading the writings of Sylvia Maxwell, an author who has written Oracle Night. Auster manages to intertwine all of these elements and blend them into one engaging plot, and, at points, the plot of the writings bleed into the real life aspects of the story. <br /><br />It’s really hard to go into particulars about plot with a Paul Auster novel, because sometimes I think that the story or meaning under the surface is far more impressionable and meaningful that the surface level plot. However, to give some context, Oracle Night’s premise involves novelist Sydney Orr having recently been released from the hospital after overcoming a near fatal illness, and his transition back into life with his wife and his friend, an author named John. There’s an interesting scene where Orr visits a stationary shop and buys a blue notebook and this sort of becomes his inspiration to begin to draft a story. There are many parallels to the story he is devising and his real life, and certain things seem to take on a life of their own. <br /><br />Within Auster’s work, there are certain elements that the author investigates, one of which is the element of how future events work. In many ways, Oracle Night is a mediation on life and its struggles, and how quite often the literary and literal world aren’t as far apart as we imagine.<br /><br />There are many layers to this novel, and this is a book that will have you reflecting after you read the final page, which I think is the hallmark of an engaging and thoughtful book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11919680982456194178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_185168562" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185168562" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185168562?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/96059.Oracle_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=library-books">library-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2000s-release">2000s-release</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185168562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4515123048629278958">'If you have never read Auster before,' proclaims a quote displayed on the cover of this edition of <i>Oracle Night</i>, 'this is the place to start.' I hadn't read Auster before beginning this book, but I'd been meaning to for a while - especially since I've now read three of his wife (Siri Hustvedt)'s books and have gathered that their work ie very similar in style and theme. I wasn't out looking for an Auster book, I was just browsing at the library, but when I spotted it, the intriguing summary and</span>
  <span id="freeText4515123048629278958" style="display:none">'If you have never read Auster before,' proclaims a quote displayed on the cover of this edition of <i>Oracle Night</i>, 'this is the place to start.' I hadn't read Auster before beginning this book, but I'd been meaning to for a while - especially since I've now read three of his wife (Siri Hustvedt)'s books and have gathered that their work ie very similar in style and theme. I wasn't out looking for an Auster book, I was just browsing at the library, but when I spotted it, the intriguing summary and that quote were good enough for me.<br /> <br /><i>Oracle Night</i> is short, but packed with detail. It's a multi-layered story, beginning with Sidney Orr - a novelist who is recovering from a severe illness - buying a unique Portuguese notebook in a rather odd stationery store. On the recommendation of his friend, also a novelist, Sidney begins to flesh out an idea for a story concerning a man who suffers a near-death experience and impulsively leaves his wife and home, resolving to start his life anew in a different city. The narrative follows both the progression of this tale and its protagonist Nick Bowen, and the 'real' story of Sidney, whose relationship with his wife Grace (the history of which is detailed in a number of footnotes) begins to flounder soon after he acquires the notebook. Meanwhile, Sidney attempts to re-write HG Wells' <i>The Time Machine</i> as a modern film script, turning it into an unconventional romance, and the Nick narrative also has a further strand wherein the character is profoundly affected by the contents of a lost manuscript, the title of which is <i>Oracle Night</i>. Like I said, multi-layered.<br /> <br />There are definitely elements of the weird about this story - the disappearance and relocation of the Paper Palace and its enigmatic proprietor, the 'powers' of the notebook - but it isn't a paranormal or fantasy novel. This really appealed to me - I love the combination of literary prose and hints of the unexplained. I also LOVED the writing. It <b>is</b> very like Hustvedt's, though it's also quite easy to tell the difference. Despite all the intricacies of the plot, it often seems secondary to the way the story is told, the ideas it explores. There are parallels galore and the book often touches on the relationship between fiction and reality and/or language and action.<br /> <br />I'd have liked this book to be longer (and it easily could have been), but overall it was a fantastic read which piqued my interest in Auster enough for me to go straight on to another of his books - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/431.The_New_York_Trilogy" title="The New York Trilogy by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">The New York Trilogy</a> - after finishing it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4515123048629278958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1279031515" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1279031515" />
    <a title="Rebecca Gransden" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24454907-rebecca-gransden"><img alt="Rebecca Gransden" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445288780p2/24454907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1279031515?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca Gransden" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca Gransden" href="/user/show/24454907-rebecca-gransden">Rebecca Gransden</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1279031515" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5059407425389453614">Auster here takes story-telling and uses that as a means to ponder reality and every labyrinthine way this translates through perception. He takes the maze and straightens it out so that every turn, every fork, every dead end corridor or way in or out is set parallel. Then what is left is something like the strata of rock, each layer signifying different eras in soil but here the unknowable passage we take from our point of view, fractured, subjective and incomplete. Auster takes that sample in </span>
  <span id="freeText5059407425389453614" style="display:none">Auster here takes story-telling and uses that as a means to ponder reality and every labyrinthine way this translates through perception. He takes the maze and straightens it out so that every turn, every fork, every dead end corridor or way in or out is set parallel. Then what is left is something like the strata of rock, each layer signifying different eras in soil but here the unknowable passage we take from our point of view, fractured, subjective and incomplete. Auster takes that sample in stone and removes it from its original position, cuts out a slab, showing each layer stark in its particular colour and bleeding into every other, and polishes it until it reflects a clear light. Then he lays it down flat so that there is no point of orientation, we can't make out the bottom from the top, but we can glide over its slippery surface taking great care and occasionally glance down at some weird reflection.<br /><br />The way in of the story within a story within a story within a going on for infinity is almost the least relevant aspect when experiencing this novel. Sure, it is the point of entry, the conceit, but that is not what is delivered whilst reading this. This is not a puzzle waiting to be solved, or a challenge, or somewhere to find solidity. I feel I float through the best of what he does and it is difficult to get a handle on what is being reached for. In The New York Trilogy Auster writes:<br /><br />"It was something like the word 'it' in the phrase 'it is raining' or 'it is night'. What that 'it' referred to Quinn had never known."<br /><br />And that is where I find this novel: walking hand in hand with the 'it'. For all the urban mysticism, the disambiguation folding back on itself, the necessary clarity of prose, the deft straddling of psychological nuance and the conveyance of time and perception relative to existence, I'm none the wiser. Perhaps that is precisely 'it'.<br /><br />I have added a blue notebook to my Christmas wishlist.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5059407425389453614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3186511566" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3186511566" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3186511566?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780786265053" href="/book/show/910454.Oracle_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=newbury-library">newbury-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=mysteries">mysteries</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-for-blog">reviewed-for-blog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=writers-and-writing">writers-and-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3186511566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6746480727700343394">This reminded me most of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3173884059' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Locked Room</a>, the final volume of the New York Trilogy. There’s even a literal locked room in a book within the book by the narrator, a writer named Sidney Orr. It’s 1982 and Orr is convalescing from a sudden, life-threatening illness. At a stationer’s shop, he buys a fine blue notebook from Portugal, hoping its beauty will inspire him to resume his long-neglected work. When he and his wife Grace go to visit John Trause, Grace’s lifelong family friend and a fellow nov</span>
  <span id="freeText6746480727700343394" style="display:none">This reminded me most of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3173884059" rel="nofollow noopener">The Locked Room</a>, the final volume of the New York Trilogy. There’s even a literal locked room in a book within the book by the narrator, a writer named Sidney Orr. It’s 1982 and Orr is convalescing from a sudden, life-threatening illness. At a stationer’s shop, he buys a fine blue notebook from Portugal, hoping its beauty will inspire him to resume his long-neglected work. When he and his wife Grace go to visit John Trause, Grace’s lifelong family friend and a fellow novelist, Orr learns that Trause uses the same notebooks. Only the blue ones, mind you. No other color fosters the same almost magical creativity.<br /><br />For long stretches of the novel, Orr is lost in his notebook (“I was there, fully engaged in what was happening, and at the same time I wasn’t there—for the there wasn’t an authentic there anymore”), writing in short, obsessive bursts. In one project, a mystery inspired by an incident from <i>The Maltese Falcon</i>, Nick Bowen, a New York City editor, has a manuscript called Oracle Night land on his desk. Spooked by a near-death experience, he flees to Kansas City, where he gets a job working on a cabdriver’s phone book archive, “The Bureau of Historical Preservation,” which includes a collection from the Warsaw ghetto. But then he gets trapped in the man’s underground bunker … and Orr has writer’s block, so leaves him there. Even though it’s fiction (within fiction), I still found that unspeakably creepy.<br /><br />In the real world, Orr’s life accumulates all sorts of complications over just nine September days. Some of them are to do with Grace and her relationship with Trause’s family; some of them concern his work. There’s a sense in which what he writes is prescient. “Maybe that’s what writing is all about, Sid,” Trause suggests. “Not recording events from the past, but making things happen in the future.” The novel has the noir air I’ve come to expect from Auster, while the layering of stories and the hints of the unexplained reminded me of Italo Calvino and Haruki Murakami. I even caught a whiff of <a>What I Loved</a>, the novel Auster’s wife Siri Hustvedt published the same year. (It wouldn’t be the first time I’ve spotted similar themes in husband‒wife duos’ work – cf. Jonathan Safran Foer and Nicole Krauss; Zadie Smith and Nick Laird.)<br /><br />This is a carefully constructed and satisfying novel, and the works within the work are so absorbing that you as the reader get almost as lost in them as Orr himself does. I’d rank this at the top of the Auster fiction I’ve read so far, followed closely by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3156303270" rel="nofollow noopener">City of Glass</a>.<br /><br /><br />Originally published on my blog, <a href="https://bookishbeck.wordpress.com/2020/02/22/paul-auster-reading-week-oracle-night-and-report-from-the-interior/" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookish Beck</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6746480727700343394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_590664270" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/590664270" />
    <a title="Shane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1798222-shane"><img alt="Shane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526065583p2/1798222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/590664270?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shane" href="/user/show/1798222-shane">Shane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer590664270" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17813561293609722962">Auster's favourite theme is randomness, chance. And we see it again in this novel, which is a story within a story within a story, told in Auster's favourite narrative style. Given that there is so much ground to cover in this short book, I'm not sure there is any other way but to "tell" this story. However, only the main story line heads towards any type of conclusion in this book, while the others stall midway, again, demonstrating randomness, perhaps?<br/><br/>I like this book because it deals with the</span>
  <span id="freeText17813561293609722962" style="display:none">Auster's favourite theme is randomness, chance. And we see it again in this novel, which is a story within a story within a story, told in Auster's favourite narrative style. Given that there is so much ground to cover in this short book, I'm not sure there is any other way but to "tell" this story. However, only the main story line heads towards any type of conclusion in this book, while the others stall midway, again, demonstrating randomness, perhaps?<br /><br />I like this book because it deals with the life of a writer, Sydney Orr, who has suffered a long illness and is just picking up his interrupted life. It is also a good study on plotting, for Auster weaves plot within plot in his embedded stories - quite deftly, it seems. Sometimes randomness can be a cop-out, when new plot points are introduced just to hype things up or slow things down. The late arrival of Jacob Trause, the truant son of Syney's mentor John Trause, and his suddden but drastic impact on the course of the narrative, pushes the concept of "chance" a bit too far. Auster also experiments with putting back-story and other non-essential detail into lengthy footnotes that overlap several pages and that can be read ( or not) to enhance the depth of the main storyline - this device worked for me..<br /><br />The lesson of randomness however, is quite powerful, because after the random event, all previous efforts by those affected seem trivial and unecessary.Which begs the question of whether one should strive in life or just go with the flow?<br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="17813561293609722962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72675444" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72675444" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72675444?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=21st-centurylit">21st-centurylit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72675444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4499644115949512315">And here I am back with Paul Auster and my ongoing love/hate relationship with him. What keeps drawing me back to him is his treatment of writers and the craft of writing in his stories, and I feel, through reading Auster, that I'm gaining some new insight through his characters. There have been a few sour grapes in our literary relationship, but there's this overwhelming "What is he up to now?" feeling that brings me back.<br/><br/>So here we have Sidney Orr, just released from the hospital after recover</span>
  <span id="freeText4499644115949512315" style="display:none">And here I am back with Paul Auster and my ongoing love/hate relationship with him. What keeps drawing me back to him is his treatment of writers and the craft of writing in his stories, and I feel, through reading Auster, that I'm gaining some new insight through his characters. There have been a few sour grapes in our literary relationship, but there's this overwhelming "What is he up to now?" feeling that brings me back.<br /><br />So here we have Sidney Orr, just released from the hospital after recovering from an almost fatal disease. He stops in a Brooklyn stationery store and finds a blue notebook made in Portugal which he purchases. The book seems to have a strange power, a very prophetic power, and therein lies Auster's story.<br /><br />Orr's own story is told largely through footnotes, which help the reader delve a little into his personal life and his relationship with his wife, Grace, and their mutual friend and fellow author, John Trause. As in any true friendship, mutual as it may be, real life sometimes gets in the way and complications arise. Therein lies another part of Auster's story.<br /><br />On top of that, the story that Orr starts writing in his new blue notebook is somewhat a contemporary retelling of <i>The Maltese Falcon</i>, encouraged by Trause. But the story Orr writes and his own life intertwine and therein lies yet another part of Auster's story.<br /><br />I love the overlapping stories, and the footnotes, and the characters. There's a discussion on color psychology which was fun in an education on symbolism in literature, if one wants to go into it that much, which of course I did.<br /><br />And so from here I feel comfortable with Auster again, and will pick up another down the line I'm sure until I hit another sour grape. And then I'll pout about it until I trick myself into reading something else by him, and etc.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4499644115949512315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14904034854896207545">There aren't many books that make a serious attempt to explore synchronicity, but this is one. I think Sidney goes from a rationalist to a fabulist/mystic though is own limitations often keep him from knowing what he knows. His happiness for being alive at the end of the book is more felt than understood and I liked that; also, that the book self refers to the Trause note which is basically to look for another notebook (of which the novel is the product 20 years later) that allows Sidney to begi</span>
  <span id="freeText14904034854896207545" style="display:none">There aren't many books that make a serious attempt to explore synchronicity, but this is one. I think Sidney goes from a rationalist to a fabulist/mystic though is own limitations often keep him from knowing what he knows. His happiness for being alive at the end of the book is more felt than understood and I liked that; also, that the book self refers to the Trause note which is basically to look for another notebook (of which the novel is the product 20 years later) that allows Sidney to begin to understand the mystery of his being.<br /><br />What I also really liked about this novel is it stimulated my own thinking about a number of themes including feeling trapped (o, the day day job), the fantasy of walking away from it all (flitcraft) and the realization that without any integrated change, you'll repeat the same pattern, etc.<br /><br />thanks to Paul Auster (and Siri) for a wonderful read.</span>

<div id="review_2908487119" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2908487119" />
    <a title="Lee Kofman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37486301-lee-kofman"><img alt="Lee Kofman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560237201p2/37486301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2908487119?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Kofman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Kofman" href="/user/show/37486301-lee-kofman">Lee Kofman</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2908487119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5499125704118857347">Paul Auster has been too prolific for his own good. Some writers just perhaps need to if not stop writing than at least slow down. I so loved his earlier novels, but Oracle Night is yet another in the string of his more recent books that reads like a soul-less repetition of his once-soulful works. This novel felt to me as being with no centre, just a bag of curiosities with characters that failed to engage me and little mysteries sawn throughout that failed to be solved. The ending was contrived</span>
  <span id="freeText5499125704118857347" style="display:none">Paul Auster has been too prolific for his own good. Some writers just perhaps need to if not stop writing than at least slow down. I so loved his earlier novels, but Oracle Night is yet another in the string of his more recent books that reads like a soul-less repetition of his once-soulful works. This novel felt to me as being with no centre, just a bag of curiosities with characters that failed to engage me and little mysteries sawn throughout that failed to be solved. The ending was contrived. Of course this being Paul Auster it wasn’t completely terrible and some scenes and observations, particularly about the nature of writing and storytelling, were amusing. But never particularly moving. I finished the book thinking ‘So what? What was the point of telling this story?’ I never felt like this with some of his other books, like the wonderful Leviathan which I read twice.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2844378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2844378" />
    <a title="Kevin Fanning" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/178078-kevin-fanning"><img alt="Kevin Fanning" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424037313p2/178078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2844378?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Fanning" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Fanning" href="/user/show/178078-kevin-fanning">Kevin Fanning</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2844378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer363721186012885540">I don't know what to say about this book. I liked. If you look at how much I liked it w/r/t how low my hopes were before starting it, I guess you could say I really liked it. But all things being equal, I mainly liked it.<br/><br/>I think if I'd read this in college I would have wanted to write a paper about it. Although, maybe not because I feel like the book is in despearate need of critical exploration, maybe just because it seems like the kind of book that a college student could fairly easily write a</span>
  <span id="freeText363721186012885540" style="display:none">I don't know what to say about this book. I liked. If you look at how much I liked it w/r/t how low my hopes were before starting it, I guess you could say I really liked it. But all things being equal, I mainly liked it.<br /><br />I think if I'd read this in college I would have wanted to write a paper about it. Although, maybe not because I feel like the book is in despearate need of critical exploration, maybe just because it seems like the kind of book that a college student could fairly easily write a paper about. There are so many stories within the stories, you can draw all kinds of parallels between them without to much fuss. You would definitely get a B, at least, and still make it to that party on Saturday.<br /><br />Like I said, I don't know what to say about this book. <br /><br />I really liked what the author did with the story he was writing in the notebook, and how it ended up. I *really* liked the part where the character in the story turns out to be friends with the same author that the character telling the story is friends with. I was hoping that would lead to all kinds of mysterious back and forth between the stories, and a sudden litany of questions about what's real and what's the story, but this turned out to not be that kind of book. Ah well. <br /><br />I thought the narrator was kind of slow in seeing what was going on, so in a sense his stunning conclusion at the end was a confirmation of what I'd been thinking all along. But that's not bad writing, that's a bad narrator. Which is good writing. <br /><br />Anyways I liked this. Probably I'll add more stars later.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="363721186012885540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_862695462" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/862695462" />
    <a title="GoldGato" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6401606-goldgato"><img alt="GoldGato" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407995357p2/6401606.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/862695462?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="GoldGato" class="user" itemprop="url" name="GoldGato" href="/user/show/6401606-goldgato">GoldGato</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780805073201" href="/book/show/458.Oracle_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6401606-goldgato?shelf=autumn">autumn</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6401606-goldgato?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer862695462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1455797367527731531"><br/>Everytime I picked up this book, I thought of that old Police song about synchronicity.<br/><br/><i>Many miles away there's a shadow on the door<br/>Of a cottage on the shore<br/>Of a dark Scottish lake</i><br/><br/>Every action seems to have a matching coincidence in this volume of wonky fiction. A stationery store suddenly appears where it didn't appear before, selling mysterious Portuguese notebooks. Characters mention trips to Portugal. The stationery store disappears overnight, then re-appears in a different location. <br/><br/><i>Many mil</i></span>
  <span id="freeText1455797367527731531" style="display:none"><br />Everytime I picked up this book, I thought of that old Police song about synchronicity.<br /><br /><i>Many miles away there's a shadow on the door<br />Of a cottage on the shore<br />Of a dark Scottish lake</i><br /><br />Every action seems to have a matching coincidence in this volume of wonky fiction. A stationery store suddenly appears where it didn't appear before, selling mysterious Portuguese notebooks. Characters mention trips to Portugal. The stationery store disappears overnight, then re-appears in a different location. <br /><br /><i>Many miles away something crawls to the surface<br />Of a dark Scottish loch</i> <br /><br />This is a picture within a picture within a picture. It's an Orson Welles-ian trip through New York City, with a synchronistic partnership with H.G. Wells. And then those damn Portuguese notebooks appear again, described so lovingly that I wanted one, too.<br /><br /><i>...when I held the notebook in my hands for the first time, I felt something akin to physical pleasure, a rush of sudden, incomprehensible well-being.</i><br /><br />A writer, still recovering from a near-fatal illness, starts writing again and finds his world falling apart. Or perhaps it's his imagination running away with him. Through the first part of the book, I wanted to scream. Just when I would get interested in a path, a door would close and that path was gone. I'm not crazy about the world of New York writers and their insular lives and then the author maintains a <i>parallel</i> narrative in the footnotes, making it all rather difficult to read. Paranormal.<br /><br />Then the second half of the book had me turning pages so fast, I could barely fathom what I was reading. Turn the page, turn the page...what the heck?...turn the page, turn the page...no way, that is NOT about to happen...turn the page, turn the page. And who is that Mr. Chang dude?<br /><br />So, 2 stars for 50% of the book. 4 stars for the remaining 50% of the book. That evens out to 3 stars overall. But I still don't think I can explain this shadow on the door.<br /><br />Book Season = Autumn (NYC mingled in pain)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1455797367527731531" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/643005564?book_show_action=true">Jun 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry" href="/user/show/12642237-terry">Terry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer643005564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4043522933486642724">That's one of the worst books I've ever read: it's the first book by Paul Auster I've read (and it will be the last one too). Auster has no clue how much his characters are superficial, mean, materialistic, opportunistic, selfish, almost sociopath and lacking of any basic human value (love, family, marriage, friendship, loyalty, trust are just empty words to any of them). 'till the end, I hoped that characters lacked of any positive side on purpose, but I was wrong. Auster had no clue, I bet he </span>
  <span id="freeText4043522933486642724" style="display:none">That's one of the worst books I've ever read: it's the first book by Paul Auster I've read (and it will be the last one too). Auster has no clue how much his characters are superficial, mean, materialistic, opportunistic, selfish, almost sociopath and lacking of any basic human value (love, family, marriage, friendship, loyalty, trust are just empty words to any of them). 'till the end, I hoped that characters lacked of any positive side on purpose, but I was wrong. Auster had no clue, I bet he thought to have pictured deep, caring, lovely, all round characters, and not the "summa" of what a human being SHOULD NEVER be. <br />After more than a year, this novel and its characters are still in my nightmares. I must admit the characters are quite realistic, because I have met such people in my life, BUT I've never read a writer who has no idea that people like he writes about are superficial, mean, materialistic, opportunistic, selfish etc. (see above). <br /><br />The style is good, do not get me wrong, but the characters really frighten me. Think to the worst and that's the half. <br /><br />EDIT. The plot include a novel into a novel, a tacky deus ex machina, the idea of writing as medicine/therapy, and a couple love stories (hahahaha). However, every aspect of the plot is not well developed, or well balanced. The main love story doesn't exist because the characters are simply not able to love anybody, but they fancy themselves in love. The secondary plot was more interesting than the primary one but it ended into a blur, while the primary plot ended into a farce. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4043522933486642724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1186325734" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marina (Sonnenbarke)" href="/user/show/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke">Marina (Sonnenbarke)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/96059.Oracle_Night">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7800917-marina-sonnenbarke?shelf=_literary-fiction">_literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1186325734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15294474937172561183">What can you say after you've finished reading a book by Paul Auster? This is the third I read by him, and it might even be the most puzzling. To come back to my question: there's nothing to say, there's so much to say.<br/><br/>Nothing to say because you just gape at the wall and say "Wow, such a wonderful book!". But at the same time there's so much to say because Auster's books are so rich you could write a paper on them, and a lengthy one at that.<br/><br/>I won't go analyzing this novel, I'm not the right pers</span>
  <span id="freeText15294474937172561183" style="display:none">What can you say after you've finished reading a book by Paul Auster? This is the third I read by him, and it might even be the most puzzling. To come back to my question: there's nothing to say, there's so much to say.<br /><br />Nothing to say because you just gape at the wall and say "Wow, such a wonderful book!". But at the same time there's so much to say because Auster's books are so rich you could write a paper on them, and a lengthy one at that.<br /><br />I won't go analyzing this novel, I'm not the right person to do that. I'll just say that there is a book within a book within a book. Therefore, a story within a story within a story, but then the stories are not only three, there's more than that. Those three are the matrioschkas, then there are parallel stories within the main story. There are footnotes, not tons of them, but still. Everyone who knows me is aware that I hate footnotes in novels. Everyone knows I hate such writers as David Foster Wallace. Though at the same time, some of you might know I loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24800.House_of_Leaves" title="House of Leaves by Mark Z. Danielewski" rel="nofollow noopener">House of Leaves</a>. And this novel by Auster had me thinking of both the novel by Danielewski and the ones by DFW. Both authors draw heavily from Paul Auster, I think.<br /><br />The stories are all very intriguing - I must say, though, that I was more drawn towards the story within the main story, that is, the story written by the protagonist, Sidney Orr, and that has Nick Bowen as the main character. That one was superb. That's probably one of the reasons why Auster leaves it unfinished. Anyway, all the stories were great, main one included. And the writing. What can I say about the writing? Other than that it's wonderful? No, I really can't say any more than that.<br /><br />Do not read this novel if you don't like postmodernism. If you do, what are you waiting for? Go for it - now!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15294474937172561183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2636687816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1967151552221757226">I don't know what it is that makes Auster's novels so compelling. I mean, he's a smartie that's for sure, and this has all the usual stories within stories within stories, the untied threads at the end, that meta playfulness he's so good at..but that never stops it from being really real and human and full of real life pain and love and anxieties. Maybe there are some aspects of this novel that feel slightly dated, but honestly this was kinda brilliant.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

It is magical. It is a bit of a tragedy. It it is a story within a story. And the writing! I'm sorry to say that I have never heard of nor read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/296961.Paul_Auster" title="Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul Auster</a> before, but I will read more of his work in the future.<br /> <br />The formatting with the footnotes was a bit strange at first, since the foot notes went on for several pages, and added needed information to the story line. <div id="review_461841014" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/461841014" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1884982-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/461841014?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/1884982-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=prose">prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=1970-present">1970-present</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer461841014" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16360049620554733653">I'm starting to really understand why some people who like Auster well enough don't necessarily look forward to the upcoming Auster book, and think that he should probably take an extended break once in a while, or stop writing novels, period. That probably sounds like a flippant remark, a mark of real dissatisfaction with at least this Auster novel, but it's not, really. <br/><br/>Because I like Auster, and I have no problem with artists who endlessly explore permutations of themes and characters that fa</span>
  <span id="freeText16360049620554733653" style="display:none">I'm starting to really understand why some people who like Auster well enough don't necessarily look forward to the upcoming Auster book, and think that he should probably take an extended break once in a while, or stop writing novels, period. That probably sounds like a flippant remark, a mark of real dissatisfaction with at least this Auster novel, but it's not, really. <br /><br />Because I like Auster, and I have no problem with artists who endlessly explore permutations of themes and characters that fascinate them, instead of attempting to be "fresh," whatever that's supposed to mean. The downside of that approach, though, is that certain books are inevitably going to seem like too much of the same thing, even if they actually aren't. <i>Invisible</i>, written well after this book, in 2009, treads familiar Austerian character and thematic terrain, and his prose style is pretty uniform across most of his work, but <i>Invisible</i> is pretty fucking good. <i>Oracle Night</i> is just good. I could rate it either the goodreads 2 star [it was ok] or the goodreads 3 star [liked it]. In general, it's pretty forgettable. I just finished it, and don't remember a lot of it, though it was compelling while I was reading it, and some images and passages linger. <br /><br />Paul Auster does what he does well. Sometimes he does it very well. I've yet to come across any duds in Paul Auster's fiction, or even real mediocrities by the broader literary fiction scene's standards, but books like this one and <i>Sunset Park</i> and <i>The Brooklyn Follies</i> aren't particularly memorable, and I suspect several of the Auster books I haven't read are of similar quality. I also suspect there are some Auster books I haven't read that are on par with <i>The New York Trilogy</i>, <i>Invisible</i>, or his film <i>Smoke</i>. In my experience thus far, it never hurts to pick up a book by Auster, but it's also rarely the kind of exquisite literary reward you want it to be, the kind of reward the last three titles I mentioned above are. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16360049620554733653" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
